What is a hashtable?
A hashtable is a symbol table that applies a hash function on a given key to look up the corresponding value in the table

Enter
Inserts a particular value for a specified key.
If an old value exists for the key, the old value is overwritten.
We need not know where the pair is added in the hashtable.

Look up
Retrieves the corresponding value for a specified key.
The specified key may not exist at all (no value exists for this key then).
We need not know how the key is found.

drs/downrev.xmlPK
Collision in Hashing
Perfection rarely exists. A hash function can sometimes return the same index (called hash code) for two different keys.
Having two or more keys hashed to the same index is called collision.
Chaining
Collision can be resolved by chaining, i.e., using a linked list for each bucket.

Hashtable Implementation (Version 1.0)
hashtableADT EmptyHashtable() {
   int i;
   hashtableADT table;
   table = (hashtableADT)malloc(sizeof(struct hashtableCDT));
   for (i = 0; i < 101; i++)
      table->buckets[i] = NULL;
   return table;
hashtable.c (continue)

Hashtable Implementation (Version 1.0)
Before writing the Enter and Lookup functions, we write a helper function FindCell.
cellT *FindCell(cellT *cp, char *key);
Traverses the linked list starting from the cell pointed to by cp.
Returns a pointer to a cell whose key matches the string pointed to by key.
Returns NULL if key is unmatched for all cells.

drs/downrev.xmlPK
cellT *FindCell(cellT *cp, char *key) {
   while (cp != NULL && strcmp(cp->key, key) != 0)
      cp = cp->next;
   return cp;
hashtable.c (continue)

[Content_Types].xmlPK
_rels/.relsPK
drs/downrev.xmlPK
void Enter(hashtableADT table, char *key, void *value) {
   cellT *cp;
   int i;
   i = Hash(key, 101);
   cp = FindCell(table->buckets[i], key);
   if (cp == NULL) {
      cp = (cellT *)malloc(sizeof(cellT));
      cp->key = (char *)malloc((strlen(key) + 1)
                               * sizeof(char));
      strcpy(cp->key, key);
      cp->next = table->buckets[i];
      table->buckets[i] = cp;
   }
   cp->value = value;
hashtable.c (continue)

[Content_Types].xmlPK

drs/downrev.xmlPK
void Enter(hashtableADT table, char *key, void *value) {
   cellT *cp;
   int i;
   i = Hash(key, 101);
   cp = FindCell(table->buckets[i], key);
   if (cp == NULL) {
      cp = (cellT *)malloc(sizeof(cellT));
      cp->key = (char *)malloc((strlen(key) + 1)
                               * sizeof(char));
      strcpy(cp->key, key);
      cp->next = table->buckets[i];
      table->buckets[i] = cp;
   }
   cp->value = value;
hashtable.c (continue)

[Content_Types].xmlPK

drs/downrev.xmlPK
void *Lookup(hashtableADT table, char *key) {
   cellT *cp;
   int i;
   i = Hash(key, 101);
   cp = FindCell(table->buckets[i], key);
   if (cp == NULL)   // unmatched key
      return NULL;   // return NULL for unmatched key
   return cp->value;   // key matched, return the value
hashtable.c (continue)

Choosing a Hash Function
The final component is the implementation of the magic hash function.
#define MULTIPLIER -16644117991L
int Hash(char *s, int nBuckets) {
   int i;
   unsigned long hashCode;
   hashCode = 0;
   for (i = 0; s[i] != '\0'; i++)
      hashCode = hashCode * MULTIPLIER + s[i];
   return (hashCode % nBuckets);
hashtable.c (continue)
Choosing a Hash Function
This is typical of the functions most often used in commercial practice.

Choosing a Hash Function
#define MULTIPLIER -16644117991L
int Hash(char *s, int nBuckets) {
   int i;
   unsigned long hashCode;
   hashCode = 0;
   for (i = 0; s[i] != '\0'; i++)
      hashCode = hashCode * MULTIPLIER + s[i];
   return (hashCode % nBuckets);

Hash Functions
A good hash function should
reduce the number of collisions;
evenly distributes the keys into buckets;
quick to compute.

Primary Clustering in Linear Probing

_rels/.relsPK
drs/downrev.xmlPK
Linear probing tends to create long sequences of filled buckets in a hash table.
This effect is called primary clustering.
Primary clustering degrades the performance of a hash table.
Quadratic probing can be used to address the primary clustering problem.
Quadratic Probing
Quadratic Probing

Let Nbuckets = 7, h0 = 0, and F(i) = i2.
h0 = 0
h1 = (0 + 1) % 7 = 1
h2 = (0 + 4) % 7 = 4
h3 = (0 + 9) % 7 = 2
h4 = (0 + 16) % 7 = 2
h5 = (0 + 25) % 7 = 4
h6 = (0 + 36) % 7 = 1

The buckets 3, 5, and 6 are never probed, even if they are empty.
Double Hashing
Second Hash Function
One possible second hash function
Rehashing
Rehashing
When Nbuckets is changed, the hash function has to be changed also. (Why?)
All entries in the hash table have to be re-entered using the new hash function.
This process is called rehashing.
Note: rehashing is rarely necessary for most applications.
